In the light of the refusal by the Gambian Government to grant diplomatic and consular protection to the owner and crew of the Community fishing vessel Briz III, which has been illegally seized and detained for more than two months in Banjul and whose crew have been beaten and ill‐treated, what measures will the Council take to compel the Gambian Government to comply with international law, compensate the Community citizens concerned and punish those responsible for the violations of international law and ill‐treatment of Community citizens?

Answer

It is up to the Member State of whose the crew and captain are nationals to make the appropriate diplomatic démarches to ensure the consular protection of its citizens. If the Member State concerned does not have a diplomatic representation in the country concerned, the appropriate démarches could be carried out by other Member States.

Furthermore, the Member State concerned can raise the issue in the appropriate instances of the Council so that the possibility of an EU approach can be examined.

Question No 23 by Gary Titley (H−0780/98)

Subject: Enacting Regulation 1475/95 on cars and other EU rules on competition

What action is the Council taking to ensure that Regulation (EC) 1475/95(1) on car distribution, and similar measures aimed at opening up EU markets to greater competition, are in fact being enforced by each Member State? What further steps does it think are needed to help consumers facing substantial price differences across the EU for the same product, for example car prices?

Question No 24 by Gerard Collins (H−0818/98)

Subject: Price differences in cars in the EU

The latest periodical study published by the Commission on differences in net prices of cars in twelve of the EU Member States (excluding Denmark, Greece and Finland) shows that price differences from one Member State to the next for the same model remain high, that difficulties remain for consumers who wish to buy right‐hand‐drive cars in cheaper markets and it recommends that consumers who are refused cars by car dealers because they reside in another country should report their problem to national authorities and the courts.

What action does the Council favour to ensure a fair price to consumers?

Joint answer

As the honourable Member will certainly be aware, Regulation (EEC) No 1475/95, which belongs to the category of group exemptions, contains certain provisions governing distribution agreements in the motor vehicle industry. It aims to ensure fairer contractual relations between manufacturers, dealers and end consumers. It is accordingly a matter for the Commission to take any measures it considers necessary to guarantee correct compliance with the regulation by the undertakings concerned and thereby protect consumers. The Commission may likewise take any practical initiatives it considers serve the purpose of greater price transparency in the motor vehicle sector.

Subject: The fight against child pornography

Can the Council state what specific action it intends to take to cut back or stop the production of, and trade in, child pornography and other forms of child abuse in Europe and, if possible, throughout the world?

Answer

‐ the Joint Action of 16 December 1996, by which the mandate of the ‘Europol Drugs Unit" has been extended to preventing and combating trafficking in human beings;

‐ and last – but not least – the work done in the framework of the Council of Europe, the United Nations (Convention for the suppression of the traffic in persons and of the exploitation of the prostitution of others, New York, 21.3.1950) the G8 and Interpol (Group on crime against minors).

Question No 26 by Per Gahrton (H−0786/98)

Subject: EU practice as regards visitors from Taiwan

In mid‐August this year, the Swedish authorities refused to grant an entry visa to the deputy prime minister of Taiwan, who was due to take part in a scientific conference in his capacity as a research scientist. The reason given by the Swedish Ministry of Foreign Affairs was that it was following EU practice not to grant visas to representatives of Taiwan travelling either in an official or in a private capacity. Inquiries revealed that the Swedish government was unable to say which Council decision was being referred to.

In view of this incident, will the Council state whether it has laid down any common practice concerning the granting of entry visas to people from Taiwan? If so, when and in what form, and what is the date and reference number of the decision? And what precisely does the practice entail, for instance what categories of persons does its cover and what categories of persons from Taiwan are to be refused entry? Does the Council consider the Swedish government's actions to be in line with any current EU practice? Are there any indications that the government of the People's Republic of China would react unfavourably if a visa were granted to someone from Taiwan for the purpose of taking part in a scientific conference?

Answer

The Union and all Member States pursue and stick closely to a ‘one country, two systems policy' on Taiwan while continuing and developing economic and commercial exchanges with Taipei.

Taiwan continues to be a thorny issue for the Chinese Government. Last year, EU Member States and institutions had to deal with Chinese demarches over visits, stop‐overs and kinds of arrangements that Taiwanese officials had been granted in Europe. This is neither new nor did it cause problems with Beijing. Nevertheless it is indicative of the fact that Beijing does not miss one single occasion to bring home its views on Taiwan.

The decision in question by the Swedish, and also by the Danish and Finnish, competent authorities to refuse entry visas seem to have been based on, and in full accordance with the text of a European Political Cooperation decision of 7 July 1988.

The text of the Decision reads in its relevant parts:

'Member States, pursuing explicitly or implicitly a ‘one China' policy, do not recognise Taiwan as a state and have no official relation with it. Some of the Member States – mainly those which had had diplomatic relations with the ‘Republic of China' before entering into diplomatic relations with the People's Republic of China – have explicitly recognized that the government of the People's Republic of China – have explicitly recognized that the government of the People's Republic of China is the only legal government of China. Other Member States have implicitly recognized the PRC government as the only Chinese Government. In accordance with this policy, the governments of Member States will avoid official encounters of their government agents with Taiwanese officials as well as any other official communication with the authorities in Taiwan'. It furthermore states that Member States shall ‘ensure the non‐official character of visits by inhabitants of Taiwan to their country and of their citizens to Taiwan.'

Finally, the agreed text makes it clear that, within the framework of the limitations described, 'all Member States permit active economic, cultural, scientific and human contacts with the inhabitants of Taiwan.'

All Member States have taken note of this decision so as to ensure, as far as possible, a common position in their attitude towards the Taiwanese authorities.

It should be added that all partners have agreed to review their policy practices towards Taiwan in the months to come. It is, however, not expected that this review will affect the established one‐China policy.

Question No 29 by Willy De Clercq (H−0792/98)

Subject: Voting rights for the European elections which must be granted, pursuant to the Treaty, to all EU subjects

The resolution in the report on citizenship of the Union, adopted by the European Parliament at its sitting of 2 July 1998, requests ‘the Member States and the Community institutions particularly to ensure that the rights to vote and stand as a candidate in elections to the European Parliament are recognized and can be exercised by all citizens who in accordance with Article 8 hold the nationality of a Member State and reside in the Union'. The Government of the United Kingdom has declared that it recognizes as its nationals ‘British Dependent Territories citizens who acquire their citizenship from a connection with Gibraltar' (OJ C 23, 28.1.1983, p. 1). Why are residents of Gibraltar still excluded from participation in the European elections, and will the Council take any action in this respect before the June 1999 elections?

Answer

The honourable Member of the Parliament certainly knows that Annex II to the 1976 Act concerning the election of representatives of the European Parliament by direct universal suffrage, which forms an integral part of the said Act, provides that ‘the United Kingdom will apply the provision of [the] Act only in respect of the United Kingdom'. This means that the 1976 Act does not apply to Gibraltar which is not part of the United Kingdom.

The 1976 Act was adopted on the basis of Article 138(3) of the Treaty, following a proposal drawn up by the European Parliament which did not mention the case of Gibraltar.

Question No 30 by María Izquierdo Rojo (H−0796/98)

Subject: Libya and Euro‐Mediterranean policy

What prospects could now be opened up for bringing consideration of Libya into Euro‐Mediterranean policy?

Answer

The Barcelona Process has been conceived as an open, inclusive initiative, and due to its very nature is in principle open to all Mediterranean countries.

At the same time it is the established position of the EU that Libya cannot join the Euro‐Mediterranean partnership until she has complied fully with the provisions of UNSCR 1192 and all other relevant Security Council resolutions.

Latest developments in the Lockerbie case have opened up a positive prospective for normalising relations between Libya and the international community.

In this context I draw the attention of the honourable Member to the recent declaration of the Presidency on behalf of the European Union in which it welcomes the readiness of the Governments of the United Kingdom and the United States to accept a trial before a Scottish Court under Scottish law in the Netherlands and the offer to allow international observers to attend the trial.

The EU regrets that the Libyan Government, in spite of encouraging initial signals has so far not reacted in a more decisive, positive way which could open the way to a re‐evaluation of EU‐Libya relations and the participation of Libya in the Barcelona Process.

Question No 32 by Mark Killilea (H−0798/98)

Subject: Violence against women – specific measures

In its recent proposal for a Council Decision on a medium‐term action programme on measures relating to violence against children, young persons and women (COM(98)335 final), there is mention of specific measures relating to women, in particular the organisation of a series of campaigns including conferences and exhibitions, which are expected to get underway during the Austrian Presidency.

Can the Council outline what progress has been made on these preparations to date? How does it propose to maximise Member States' participation in such a series of measures, so that a strong EU‐wide network can be established from the outset to facilitate and encourage ongoing collection of data and exchange of information on the whole area of violence against women?

Answer

A proposal for a Council Decision on a medium‐term action programme on measures for a Community‐wide support to Member States' action relating to violence against children, young persons and women (DAPHNE programme) (2000‐2004) was referred to the Council by the Commission on 9 July 1998.

The Council will be commencing its examination of the proposal very shortly. The Austrian Presidency has stated its intention of dealing with this matter without delay.

Question No 33 by Hadar Cars (H−0800/98)

Subject: EU mine‐clearance project in Sarajevo

A few years ago, the EU decided to start up a programme for clearing mines in Sarajevo. According to reports in the Swedish press today, the project has been discontinued as the EU contribution was sufficient only to cover the training of those who would be clearing the mines and equipment and the money had run out when the work was due to begin.

Training ended in September 1997, and nothing has since happened with the project. The equipment is now sitting in a cupboard in Sarajevo, and those who were trained up are now idle. No EU official has been able to comment on this matter. Mines are killing more and more people in Sarajevo. The need is acute. What has happened to the EU mine‐clearance project?

Answer

In the overall context of the EU's de‐mining activities in Bosnia and Herzegovina in 1996 and 1997, various contracts were signed with a number of private operators to train and equip de‐miners and to carry out de‐mining operations. These countries, which were generally completed satisfactorily, came to an end in January 1998.

Considerable efforts were made to ensure the sustainability of these activities in 1998 and beyond. Two memorandums of understanding were signed with the BiH authorities defining a new institutional framework in anticipation of a gradual transfer of responsibilities. In addition, a series of contracts were signed with an NGO to continue operations and to preserve the investment already made, as well as to help implement the understanding reached with BiH side. Following planning activities that are currently under way, field operations should start within the next few weeks.

The delays referred to by the honourable Parliamentarian are therefore not due to inaction by those responsible, but rather to the need of having to respond to the inherent challenges of a difficult consolidation phase.

For details on the specific project in Sarajevo mentioned by the honourable Parliamentarian, he could address himself to the European Commission which manages the EU's activities in this field.

Question No 34 by Richard Howitt (H−0803/98)

Subject: Missing Civilian Adnan Musbah Halwani

In 1982, Adnan Musbah Halwani was working in Beirut, ensuring that such services as hospitals and schools continued to function during the Israeli invasion in 1982. He ‘disappeared' from Beirut in September 1982, reportedly taken by agents of the Lebanese Military Intelligence called ‘Al‐Shu'ba Al‐Thaniyya'. Although in 1995, the Lebanese Government issued a law to allow families to have missing people declared legally dead, the families concerned rejected this, because it did not deal with the issues of investigating the fate of the missing people, and questioning those responsible. Can the Council bring pressure to bear upon the Lebanese Government to establish the fate of Adnan Musbah Halwani, and bring to justice those responsible for his disappearance?

Answer

The Council is aware that a large number of people disappeared during the dramatic period of the civil war in Lebanon, although it does not possess further information on the case of Adnan Musbah Halwani. Unfortunately, it seems unlikely that all those responsible for disappearances and other human rights violations during that difficult period can be brought to justice.

The Union has been supporting the Lebanese reconstruction efforts to recover from the effects of a civil war which caused so much human suffering, providing 40 % of the total international assistance towards this goal.

This important commitment, together with the effective partnership launched by the Barcelona Process may be of critical importance to create the conditions for peace, security and lasting national reconciliation in Lebanon which in turn could lead to an effort to address the atrocities referred to by the honourable Members.

Question No 35 by John Cushnahan (H−0811/98)

Subject: Register of sex offenders

Would the Council give consideration to drawing up an EU‐wide register of sex offenders?

Answer

The Austrian presidency has proposed last August a Joint Action to combat child pornography, which is discussed in the Council and on which Parliament has been consulted by the presidency. Question No 36 by Anne Van Lancker (H−0813/98)

Subject: Visit by a delegation from Myanmar to France and Portugal; circumvention of the Common Position on Myanmar

In July and August representatives of the Burmese regime visited Portugal and France. In Portugal they took part in a conference of ministers responsible for youth affairs. The French visit involved contacts with industry and Total put pressure on the government to issue visas. The ban on visas for senior members, and their families, of the governing body of Myanmar, the SLORC (now the SPDC), announced in the Common Position concerning Myanmar(2) , has apparently been circumvented by the issuing of transit visas. Is the Council prepared to make this circumvention impossible by amending the Common Position accordingly? What has been the Council's response to the European Parliament's request(3) for strict application of the measures on Myanmar? Have there been similar missions to Union Member States, and were there contacts with representatives of the EU or of the governments of the Member States?

Answer

The Union agreed a Common Position on Burma/Myanmar on 28 October 1996. It was lastly extended in time on 29 April 1998 and is due for new consideration no later than 29 October.

The Common Position prohibits in its Article 5(b) (I) Member States to issue entry visas for senior members of the SPDC and their families. Article 5(b) (ii) bans entry visas for senior members of the Burmese military or security forces who formulate, implement or benefit from policies that impede Burma/Myanmar's transition to democracy, and their families. There is however no ban on transit visits.

An agreement among partners in relation to the extension of the Common Position on 29 April stipulates that a Member State considering the issuing of a transit visa to Burmese officials should inform other Member States of their intention. This procedure was adhered to by France in the case discussed.

Portugal informed Member States in due time about the invitation sent to Burma/Myanmar to attend an international conference of ministers responsible for youth affairs organised in cooperation with the United Nations. It is the Council's understanding that Member States who grant visas to any Burmese officials participating in a UN‐sponsored conference in their territory are not circumventing the ban on visas announced in the Common Position.

Question No 37 by Ioannis Theonas (H−0814/98)

Subject: Unacceptable proposal by Turkey and Mr Denktash regarding the definitive partition of Cyprus

A proposal has been put forward by Mr Cem and Mr Denktash, during the former's visit to the territory of Turkish‐occupied Cyprus, for a loose confederation of two sovereign and equal states in Cyprus. This proposal flies in the face of the Summit agreements and the countless United Nations resolutions on a single federation with two zones and two communities which are the basis for efforts to resolve the Cyprus question and secure the withdrawal of the Turkish army of occupation and the settlers.

Does the Council not consider that proposals of this kind leading to the definitive partition of Cyprus and seeking to legitimize the Turkish invasion and occupation and Mr Denktash's so‐called ‘state' reveal the intransigence and cynicism of Turkey and will it take steps to condemn Turkey's stance and Mr Denktash's ‘state' and to seek a solution to the Cypriot question based on the resolutions of the United Nations and international law?

Answer

The EU welcomes all attempts to contribute constructively to a settlement for Cyprus. However, a confederation of separate sovereign states is not consistent with the objective of a bi‐zonal, bi‐communal federation agreed by both communities in the 1977 and 1979 High Level Agreements and set out in all relevant UN Security Council resolutions, eg in UNSC Resolution 1179 of 29 June 1998.

The European Union remains fully committed to a peaceful political settlement of the Cyprus question and strongly supports the efforts of the UN Secretary‐General and his Special Advisor with a view to creating a bi‐communal, bi‐zonal federation. Accordingly the EU, with the honourable Member, deplores initiatives from whatever source which run counter to this objective.

Question No 38 by Pat Gallagher (H−0816/98)

Subject: Legal right to interest on late payments

Will the Council indicate its timetable for adopting rules providing a legal right to interest on late payments, and the extent to which small and medium‐sized enterprises in Ireland and the other Member States have been consulted and can benefit from the Commission's proposals, and will it state the likely impact on the public sector of honouring payment contract dates?

Answer

The lack of action by most Member States to tackle the problem of late payment in commercial transactions and its persistent damaging effects in such transactions have led the Commission to introduce a proposal for a directive on this issue.

Such a proposal, based on Articles 100(a) and 189(b) of the Treaty, was submitted to the Council and the European Parliament on 24 April 1998.

The Council reminds the honourable Member that at this stage the preparatory work concerning this directive has not yet been finalised either at the level of the Council or the European Parliament.

Concerning the other questions raised about this proposal, the honourable Member will find attached to the document submitted to the Council and the European Parliament a chapter entitled ‘The impact of the proposal on business, with special reference to small‐ and medium‐sized business (SMEs)", dealing inter alia with the consultation of interested parties initiated by the Commission.

Question No 39 by Brian Crowley (H−0817/98)

Subject: Lesser‐used languages

Has the Council examined the role and status of lesser‐used languages in the context of enlargement and what are the implications for EU cultural policy in the future in terms of preserving and fostering lesser‐used languages?

Answer

Due consideration would be given by the Council to any Commission proposals relative to the role and status of lesser‐used languages, including in the context of enlargement.

In terms of the current situation, it may be pointed out that several of the applicant states to the EU have become, or are in the process of becoming, participants in the Community's culture, youth, education and vocational training programmes. These are based, as appropriate, on Articles 128, 126 and 127 of the Treaty, which are referred to as follows in the Council's conclusions of 12 June 1995 on linguistic diversity and multilingualism in the European Union: ‘Linguistic diversity is a component of the national and regional diversity of cultures of the Member States referred to in Article 128 of the Treaty, and the Community must take it into account in its actions under other provisions of the Treaty, including Articles 126 and 127, which cover education, vocational training and youth." It is moreover stated in the same conclusions that ‘The Council stresses the importance of taking the language dimension into account in the Union's external relations, particularly in the framework of the Union's programmes involving third countries."

It would also be useful to recall a principle enshrined under the LINGUA action in the current education programme (SOCRATES) even though current non‐Community languages are not concerned, whereby provision shall be made for priority support ‘of the least widely used and taught languages of the European Union".

Question No 40 by Liam Hyland (H−0825/98)

Subject: Impact of Austrian's Presidency's objectives for rural development and Ireland

The programme of the Austrian Presidency indicates a desire to give special attention to the new support measures for an integrated rural development policy.

Will the President‐in‐office outline how the Austrian Presidency is pursuing this objective and how it considers its objectives will benefit the further development of an integrated rural development policy in Ireland?

Answer

1. Matters relating to rural development in the context of Agenda 2000 are currently being dealt with by the Council as constituted by the Agriculture Ministers.

During the UK Presidency, the Council carried out an initial in‐depth examination of the proposals put forward by the Commission in this field and recognised the key role that rural development policy must play in the development of European agricultural policy. The Council intends to move in the near future to the next phase of these activities and in particular to place the promotion of the development of the rural economy and environment at the centre of its deliberations; this will include multi‐functional agriculture, the appropriate degree of subsidiarity and simplification for these measures and the extent of the mechanisms and resources required for this purpose.

2. The Austrian Presidency is fully aware of the importance of these issues and the expectations they raise in every Member State. It has therefore decided, with a view also to lasting development, to place them on the agenda for the informal meeting of Agriculture Ministers to be held in St Wolfgang on 20 and 21 September 1998, in addition to the business already programmed.

It would be premature at this juncture to assess the favourable impact which this set of measures may have on any given Member State. Furthermore, Parliament has before it the Commission's proposal concerning rural development and is required to deliver its opinion in connection with its examination of Agenda 2000.

Question No 42 by Eva Kjer Hansen (H−0827/98)

Subject: Possibility of derogation from Directive 91/439/EEC

Council Directive 91/439/EEC(4) on driving licences lays down minimum standards regarding the physical and mental fitness required for driving motor vehicles. Since the Directive entered into force, many EU citizens have got into difficulties because they cannot get their driving licences renewed on the basis of the new, amended requirements and so are unable to continue with their occupations.

When the Directive was adopted, the Council did not incorporate the Commission's proposal for a clause making it possible for people to have their licences renewed under the terms and conditions that applied before the Directive entered into force.

Will the Council give its views on this issue and explain how it intends to resolve the problem so that established and practising driving instructors are not deprived of their living?

Answer

The Council did not consider it appropriate to include a specific clause in Directive 91/439/EEC allowing the Member States to make it possible for drivers who obtained their licences prior to 1 July 1990, under more lenient conditions than those laid down in this directive, to renew their licences regularly on the same conditions as when they first gained them, as the Commission had proposed.

It is the responsibility of the Member States, under the supervision of the Commission and, if necessary, the Court of Justice, to rule on the requests made by the drivers referred to by the honourable Member, as part of the application of the national measures implementing the above directive.

Question No 43 by Inger Schörling (H−0828/98)

Subject: Mobile slaughterhouses

There are no facilities in northern Sweden for slaughtering laying hens. This is because Sweden's most northerly slaughterhouse for slaughtering laying hens is in Skara, and it is not considered economical to transport laying hens to it from further north than Gästrikland. This means that laying hens from more northerly areas cannot be slaughtered and instead have to be destroyed using anti‐simex, at a cost of SEK 2 per hen. Plans have therefore been put forward for a mobile slaughterhouse which could travel between egg producers in northern Sweden and slaughter laying hens which were not longer suitable for egg production purposes. This would be justified on animal welfare grounds, as the hens would not have to be transported at all, and also on economic grounds, as the meat could then be used. However, Sweden's National Food Administration has been informed by an official at the Commission that mobile slaughterhouses are not permitted.

Is it true that mobile slaughterhouses can never be permitted? If so, how does the Council consider that the above problem might be resolved? Should the rules not take account of the circumstances that prevail in remote areas? What are the Council's views as regards the animal welfare and economic impact of the arrangements described above?

Answer

The Council can only confirm that the legislation currently in force does not permit the slaughter of poultry destined for human consumption in mobile slaughterhouses. Indeed, Article 3 of Directive 71/118/EEC stipulates quite clearly the conditions under which such meat is to be obtained. To be sold, it must originate from registered slaughterhouses, in compliance with the requirements laid down in Annex 1, Chapter 1, of Annex 1 to the above directive.

The Council has not received any proposals for derogations to allow mobile slaughterhouses in some or all of the territory of the Union.

2.Questions to the Commission

 

Question No 53 by Concepció Ferrer (H−0802/98)

Subject: Trade preferences for third countries

In May 1998 the Council adopted the revision of the regulations concerning special arrangements to stimulate activity in developing countries by means of additional preferences.

This revision should be viewed in the context of the analyses carried out by the ILO, the WTO, the OECD and the International Tropical Timber Organisation; it is an important means of promoting exports to the countries concerned, which could gain substantially from operating the measures set out in the relevant regulation.

What action has the Commission taken, or what action does it intend to take, to publicise the relevant provisions of the regulation concerned in the countries and areas of activity where there is likely to be interest in this preferential regime?

Answer

As from the entry into force of Council Regulation (EC) No 1154/98(5) , of 25 May 1998, applying the special incentive arrangements concerning labour rights and environmental protection provided for in Articles 7 and 8 of the Regulations (EC) No 3281/94(6) and (EC) No 1256/96(7) applying multiannual schemes of generalised tariff preferences (GSP) in respect of certain industrial and agricultural products originating in developing countries, the Commission convened a meeting in Brussels with the representatives of the diplomatic missions of beneficiary countries.

It also provides information to the beneficiary countries via its delegation in the third countries.

Moreover during its GSP information seminars held in the beneficiary countries on their request the Commission will put a particular emphasis on the special incentive regimes.

Furthermore, a special information campaign on these new procedures is also envisaged by the Commission to the various actors concerned (producers, importers, governments of the beneficiary countries, trade unions and NGOs performing in these countries, etc).

Question No 54 by Richard Howitt (H−0804/98)

Subject: European development cooperation and private investment

There is an increasing trend towards globalisation of international trade and cooperation. On entering new markets, European companies are facing challenges operating in countries where human rights and international environmental standards are not upheld. Increasingly, companies and donor agencies, including the European Union, are subject to criticism for failing to respect basic rights, e.g. oil companies in Ecuador, Venezuela, Peru and Colombia, the flower industry in Colombia and EU projects in Ecuador. In order to better marry the objectives of European development cooperation with European private investment, does the Commission consider it important to establish monitoring and external auditing systems which evaluate existing company codes of conduct and European and international labour and environmental standards?

Answer

The improvement of social and environmental conditions is one of the Commission's constant preoccupations. It contributes actively thereto in the developing countries, both generally, under global Community policies, and specifically, through a large number of individual projects.

The Commission follows with interest the efforts made to define and implement voluntary codes of conduct adopted by businesses or negotiated by the social partners. It takes the view that those codes may play an important role in promoting respect for fundamental social rights. Accordingly, it feels that such codes should be based on the body of recommendations and the like adopted by the International Labour Organisation and that the partners involved in defining them should cooperate in order to ensure the efficient application thereof. The establishment of an independent monitoring and verification body might indeed contribute towards promoting the implementation of fundamental standards.

For its part, the Commission has itself begun an exploratory study into the ways and means available for promoting, especially in the social field, a code of conduct for European businesses which invest in developing countries. In so doing, it reviews the terms of the existing codes and the results of their application. Its work therefore involves analysing the scope and impact of those codes rather than monitoring and auditing them as such. And, of course, since these codes of conduct are voluntary, it is not for the Commission to verify whether or not the companies involved are complying with them. At all events, the Community does not currently have a legal basis on which it could impose binding conditions.

Question No 65 by Pat Gallagher (H−0820/98)

Subject: ‘EURO HOTLINE'

As the Commission will recall, during the debate on the EURO on 13 January 1998 I called on it to agree to and finance a ‘EURO HOTLINE' to protect the interests of consumers and businesses when the EURO is introduced and to detect and stop unjustified price rices. The Commission said, in reply, that such an idea could be examined.

Will the Commission now state its present position on my proposal for a ‘EURO HOTLINE'?

Answer

As the honourable Member will recall, the Commission had supported the principle of information centres being set up to respond to the concerns and meet the requirements of consumers and businesses in connection with the introduction of the euro.

Accordingly, it has encouraged the Member States to develop information systems for their own citizens. To date, the eleven Member States which will introduce the euro on 1 January 1999 have created freephone numbers for the euro, as well as Internet sites, a list of which was published in March 1998 in the Commission's ‘Infeuro' newsletter. An updated version of that list will be published towards the end of the year.

Apart from these general information services, which help to inform consumers and businesses, the Commission has also encouraged the Member States to improve consumer protection.

In its recommendation of 23 April 1998(8) , the Commission called on the Member States ‘including where appropriate local authorities, ... to establish suitable facilities, and preferably set up observatories on the transition to the euro, for monitoring the introduction of the euro, and the fairness and transparency of professional practices.'

Those observatories should serve to identify more effectively and discourage unjustified price rises during the period of transition to the euro. They will also perform tasks relating to ‘reception, mediation and assistance to citizens'.

The establishment of such observatories was confirmed by the agreement concluded on 30 June 1998 between businessmen and consumers on the display of prices in two currencies.

In order to serve the interests of consumers in their everyday lives more effectively, these observatories should be established at local level, as part of existing administrative structures and in compliance with national law.

Question No 66 by Antoni Gutiérrez Díaz (H−0722/98)

Subject: Passenger space on board aircraft

Passenger space on economy class flights on airlines based in the EU is very often minimal. This is not only uncomfortable but can represent an additional risk in case of accident.

Does the Commission not consider that legislation is needed to ensure minimum standards of comfort and safety?

Answer

Current safety regulations for aircraft, which have been introduced into Community law through Council Regulation (EEC) No 3922/91 on the harmonisation of technical requirements and administrative procedures in the field of civil aviation, do not specifically address seating space for passengers, except insofar as aisle width and access to exits are concerned. Safety requirements are linked to the ability to evacuate all passengers in a given time in case of emergency and that, in turn, implies restrictions on the number of passengers that a given aircraft can carry, but not on seating configuration.

Seating configuration relates to comfort and remains the responsibility of aircraft operators. Beyond the existing safety regulations, therefore, the Commission has no intention to regulate issues concerning the comfort of passengers.

Question No 67 by John McCartin (H−0723/98)

Subject: Objective One status for regions in Ireland

Can the Commission state whether any consideration is being given to the proposal that under‐developed regions in Ireland be allowed to make separate application for Objective One status under the new Structural Fund arrangements to start in the year 2000?

Answer

With reference to Objective 1, the proposal for a general Structural Funds Regulation approved by the Commission on 18 March 1998 provides for the calculation of per capita GDP expressed in purchasing power, at the level of the NUTS II regions, on the basis of the figures for the three most recent years available. Only the NUTS II regions with a per capita GDP below 75 % of the Community average would be eligible for Objective 1 status during the period 2000‐2006. Following Council approval of the regulation, the Commission would draw up a list of such regions, basing its work on the division of the NUTS II regions in force at that time.

The Commission has also proposed transitional support, for a period of six or seven years, for regions currently eligible for Objective 1 status but which will not be so eligible after 1 January 2000, in order to avoid an abrupt cessation of Community aid and any jeopardising of the results achieved to date on the basis of aid from the Structural Funds.

Ireland constitutes one single NUTS II region. On the basis of the figures currently available, its per capita GDP is above the threshold of 75 % of the Community average. The entire country should therefore be covered by the phasing‐out of Objective 1 status and, accordingly, benefit from the transitional support proposed by the Commission.

Question No 68 by Michael Elliott (H−0726/98)

Subject: Wild Birds Directive

Is the Commission taking steps regarding the failure of the French authorities to properly enforce the requirements of the EC Wild Birds Directive relating to dates of the hunting season for migratory birds? Furthermore, how is it intending to respond to the recent vote in the French National Assembly unilaterally to extend the permitted hunting season?

Answer

In 1997, the Commission brought proceedings against the French Republic for infringement of the treaties on the grounds that the dates for the opening of the hunting season in that Member State did not comply with the provisions of the Community's ‘Wild Birds Directive' (Council Directive 79/409/EEC of 2 April 1979 on the conservation of wild birds). The dates for the opening and the closing of the hunting season in France cannot guarantee complete protection for some species of wild birds during their migration back to their nesting grounds or during their reproduction and nesting periods. On 24 June 1998, the Commission decided to forward a reasoned opinion to the French Republic. That constitutes the second stage of the procedure under Article 169 concerning failure to comply with the Treaty establishing the European Communities.

When it announced its decision to forward a reasoned opinion, the Commission indicated, firstly, that that procedure predated the legislative changes adopted by the French National Assembly and, secondly, that it feared that those changes, which exacerbate the shortcomings referred to above, would simply aggravate the problems connected with the hunting season in France(9) .

Question No 69 by Angela Billingham (H−0730/98)

Subject: UEFA rule on common ownership

Is the Commission aware of the great disquiet in Greece, where football fans are in uproar, caused by UEFA implementing a new rule, prohibiting football clubs under common ownership from participating in the same UEFA Cup Competition? This rule, which was introduced retrospectively and without consultation with those affected, will prevent AEK Athens FC from taking its place in the season's UEFA Cup competition.

Surely, the Commission would agree that it is unreasonable and unfair for UEFA to implement this new rule when AEK and other clubs have already qualified for the European competitions, thus destroying AEK's acquired right to participate, disappointing the players and the fans, and affecting the integrity of the overall competition?

Answer

The Commission is aware that UEFA has recently adopted rules that regulate the participation, on European competitions, of clubs belonging to the same owner. The AEK Athens FC as well as the Slavia Prague have been directly concerned by these rules as they both belong to the same group (Enic group).

As the honourable Member should be aware, the two clubs and UEFA have submitted their dispute over the new rules to the Court of Arbitration for Sport (CAS). The CAS has decided, as a preliminary relief in the case, to postpone the application of UEFA's new rules for the season 1998/99, to avoid an irreparable harm to AEK Athens.

It seems at first sight that the concerned rules have a sporting nature and that they aim to preserve uncertainty of results, an objective which the Court has recognised as legitimate in its judgment of 15 December 1995 in the Bosman case(10) .

Clearly, if clubs with the same owner can take part in the same competitions, whether national or international, doubts may arise as to whether the outcome is really undecided in advance.

To the extent that these UEFA rules would constitute a restriction of competition, it would be necessary to determine whether they are limited to what is strictly necessary to attain the objective of ensuring the uncertainty as to results or whether there exist less restrictive means to achieve it. Provided that such rules would remain in proportion to the sporting objective pursued, they would not be covered by the competition rules laid down in the EC Treaty.

At this stage, the Commission does not possess all the necessary information to definitely assess the compatibility of the rules with Articles 85 and 86 of the EC Treaty.

Question No 70 by Friedhelm Frischenschlager (H−0732/98)

Subject: Equal opportunities: social protection

At the end of January this year the liberals held a conference in Brussels on the subject of equal opportunities, which was attended by over 90 women from Austria. Matters discussed at the conference included the social protection afforded to women, which throughout Europe fails to meet the criteria for the promotion of equal opportunities.

What does the Commission intend to do in future, aside from its highly commendable efforts in the field of equal opportunities, to improve this unsatisfactory situation for those concerned?

Answer

In the area of equal treatment between women and men in the social security field, there are three Council directives, 79/7/EEC (equality in statutory social security schemes)(11) and 86/378/EEC(12) and 96/97/EC(13) (equality in occupational social security schemes).

However, there are still gaps in the implementation of the principle of equal treatment, especially in the area of statutory schemes. Directive 79/7/EEC excludes from its scope important matters such as pensionable age, survivors' benefits and family benefits.

The Commission intends to present a new proposal for a Council Directive completing the existing Community law in the area of equal treatment between women and men in social security, taking into consideration the new gender balance and the Case Law of the Court.

This proposal will replace a proposal made in 1987(14) which is still pending before the Council despite favourable opinions of the Parliament and of the Economic and Social Committee.

Question No 71 by José Apolinário (H−0741/98)

Subject: Commission initiative on ultra‐peripheral regions

The Treaty of Amsterdam includes an article on the specific problems of the island and ultra‐peripheral regions. Given that in ‘Agenda 2000' the Commission proposes putting an end to the Community initiative REGIS and makes no specific political proposals, can it explain how it intends to guarantee a policy based on economic and social cohesion for the ultra‐peripheral regions?

Answer

The proposal for a regulation on general arrangements for the Structural Funds, approved by the Commission on 18 March 1998, recognises the special circumstances of the ultra‐peripheral regions, as defined in the new Article 299 of the Treaty of Amsterdam, currently in the process of ratification.

For the period 2000‐2006, the Commission has proposed that regions where the GDP per inhabitant is less than 75 % of the Community average should be eligible under Objective 1. However, all the ultra‐peripheral regions are to be eligible under this Objective, in view of the type of problems they experience and the long‐term nature of these.

In addition, ultra‐peripheral regions within Member States eligible for the Cohesion Fund may benefit from a concessionary rate of assistance under the Structural Funds of up to 85 % of the total eligible cost.

Furthermore, any actions hitherto implemented under the Community's REGIS initiative may be proposed by the Member States as part of the assistance under Objective 1.

Question No 72 by Nuala Ahern (H−0745/98)

Subject: British Nuclear Fuels' plan to increase its nuclear fuels trade with Japan and South Korea

What assessment has the Commission made of the plans by British Nuclear Fuels plc to increase its nuclear fuels trade with Japan and South Korea? Has the Commission examined the implications for nuclear proliferation arising from the BNFL intention to export plutonium‐based MOX fuel to Japan? Is the Commission in possession of any details from BNFL or the UK Government in respect of such trade, centred on Sellafield, with regard to environmental pollution?

Answer

The Commission has regular contacts with British Nuclear Fuels plc. (BNFL) about its nuclear fuels trade. Contracts concluded by BNFL for reprocessing and/or MOX fuel fabrication are notified to the Euratom Supply Agency. On 25 May 1998, the Council approved a proposal by the Commission to negotiate an agreement with Japan on the peaceful use of nuclear energy. One of the objectives of the proposed agreements with Japan is to provide a secure long‐term framework for future nuclear commercial arrangements between undertakings in the Community and in Japan. With respect to the Republic of Korea, the Commission has no proposals to make at this stage as to an agreement on nuclear trade.

The MOX fuel referred to in the question is manufactured from material imported from Japan (and owned by a Japanese utility) as irradiated fuel, which has been reprocessed. The material has been under Euratom safeguards from the moment it arrived in the Community and remains under IAEA safeguards. Once the MOX assemblies are exported to Japan they will continue under international (IAEA) safeguards, as Japan signed a full‐scope safeguards agreement with the IAEA under the Treaty on the Non‐Proliferation of Nuclear Weapons. The material will be verified before and after shipment by the IAEA. In addition, appropriate containment and surveillance measures will be applied. Thus, the measures necessary to prevent the diversion of nuclear material to any illicit purposes will be taken in addition to the physical protection requirements.

As regards environmental pollution, the Commission is not aware of any losses of such shipments. The shipping containers meet the requirement of the IAEA regulations as adopted by the International Maritime Organization (IMO) and the ships embody numerous safety features such as reinforced double hulls and duplicated navigation, communication, electrical and cooling systems. A joint IMO/IAEA/UNEP Working Group has concluded that all of the information available demonstrates very low levels of radiological risk and environmental consequences.

Question No 73 by Doeke Eisma (H−0748/98)

Subject: Environment and the developing countries

Can the Commission indicate why it has cut 50 % of the appropriations entered against Items B7‐6201 (Tropical forests) and B7‐6200 (Environment in the developing countries)?

Can it indicate (as a percentage) how much of the current development aid budget is devoted to the environment?

Can it indicate how the integration of the environment into development policy is guaranteed?

Answer

Due to general budget constraints the Commission has to prioritise its actions. Therefore, all budget lines have been carefully examined. In the case of budget lines B7‐6200 ‘Environment in Developing Countries' and B7‐6201 ‘Tropical Forests' it was found that part of the activities, in particular project activities in the field, under these two budget lines can equally well be covered from other funding sources without compromising the development cooperation policy of the European Community. In the ACP countries there are sufficient financial resources available from the European Development Fund for which the disbursement rate can be significantly increased. Therefore, the reduction in the requested allocation by only fifty percent has to be seen as part of an exercise to mainstream and fully integrate environment into the development policy. Consequently, this is not an attempt by the Commission to reduce the importance of environment or the spending on environmental actions in the development cooperation.

This also follows the recommendations of two recently completed major and comprehensive evaluations of EC actions concerning the environment and tropical forests, respectively. On the basis of the former, a set of instructions have been issued to improve environmental performance, mainly by mainstreaming the issue into overall development policy. Concerning tropical forests, the Commission is currently preparing a Communication to the Council and the Parliament which will address in detail the different options for future actions in this field.'

Over the period 1990/95, as part of its cooperation programmes with Asian, Latin American and Mediterranean countries (ALA/MED), as well as the ACP regions (Africa, Caribbean and Pacific States), the Commission committed ECU 1 339 m to environmental projects, or 8.5 % of total funds committed over this period. The major themes on which these commitments were concentrated are the following: land resources, tropical forests, biodiversity, urban environment and institutional strengthening.

The EC has considerably increased policy work on sustainable development, in close cooperation with Member States' experts. In this context, the Commission is in the process of finalising a set of guidelines on water resources, and is actively working on biodiversity, sustainable energy and Environmental Impact Assessment.

Question No 74 by Sören Wibe (H−0750/98)

Subject: Tax‐free sales and international conventions

The EU Member States have signed international conventions stating that sales in international waters and international airspace shall be tax free. No country is entitled to impose taxes when journeys run between two States.

Does not the decision to stop tax‐free sales within the EU from 1999 constitute a breach of these conventions? Should the latter not be renegotiated with all the signatory countries?

Answer

In the Commission's view, this question relates principally to the international conventions on maritime affairs.

The Commission can confirm that the European Community, along with the Member States, has signed the International Convention on the Law of the Sea. This convention does not specifically regulate the taxation of sales on board ship, although it does stipulate, in more general terms, that no State has the right to claim sovereignty over any part of the high seas.

On the other hand, this convention does not contain any measures relating to potential duty‐free sales on the high seas.

Together with the Council's group of experts on the law of the sea, the Commission has studied the compatibility of existing Community fiscal and customs regulations with the International Convention on the Law of the Sea. No incompatibility was found.

Question No 75 by John Cushnahan (H−0788/98)

Subject: Abolition of duty‐free sales

How does the Commission reconcile the offer made by Commissioner Monti to identify the State and Community instruments available to alleviate the social and economic problems that would arise should duty‐ and tax‐free sales be abolished in mid 1999, whilst at the same time a fellow Commissioner reporting on the ‘Sixth Survey on State Aid in the EU in the manufacturing and other Sectors' is stating categorically that there is a need to reduce substantially all state aid to industry?

Is it not now time for the EU Commission to review its internal positioning before making promises it cannot deliver upon?

Answer

It is true that, on 19 May 1998, the Commission offered to make available to the ECOFIN Council a working document concerning intra‐Community tax‐free sales.

That document is designed to clarify the instruments which may be used by the Member States in order to respond to any problems which they have identified.

The Commission does not see any contradiction between that position and the approach of principle that it has adopted as regards state aids.

The abolition of tax‐free sales within the Community after 30 June 1999 will result in the abolition of global support, in the form of a fiscal measure, for one business sector which is currently borne by all taxpayers.

On the other hand, the use of national or Community instruments will be designed to alleviate, within the framework of existing Community legislation, any – closely defined – social and economic problems by means of instruments defined or established for that purpose.

Accordingly, the result will be an overall reduction in the support currently provided and better targeting of any measures to be taken.

Question No 76 by Alex Smith (H−0754/98)

Subject: Radioactive and toxic waste

Following the agreement at the ministerial meeting of the Ospar Convention on the control of discharges to sea of radioactive and toxic waste, held at Sintra in Portugal in July, what steps is the Commission taking to increase the monitoring capability of Euratom to ensure that liquid radioactive discharges from Sellafield and Dounreay in the UK and La Hague in France do not continue to pollute the coastlines and fisheries of these nations, plus Spain, Ireland and the Scandinavian countries?

Answer

The responsibility for the control of discharges to sea of radioactive waste remains with Member States. Under the terms of Article 36 of the Euratom Treaty the Commission is informed of the levels of radioactivity in Member States (e.g. Ireland, Spain) including, where appropriate, along the coastline. It also receives information from countries such as Norway. The Commission examines such information in the light of the Basic Safety Standards for the protection of the health of workers and the general public against the dangers arising from ionizing radiation (Directive 80/836/Euratom(15) ; Directive 96/29/Euratom(16) to be implemented in May 2000).

Under the terms of Article 35 of the Euratom Treaty, the Commission also has a right of access to the facilities installed by Member States to monitor levels of radioactivity in the environment. It can verify their operation and efficiency. Verification missions were carried out in the UK (Dounreay and Sellafield, in 1993), around La Hague (France, 1996) and in Ireland (1996). The airborne and liquid discharge monitoring procedures of the three reprocessing plants concerned were subject to verification.

A Communication to the Council and to the European Parliament on the application of Article 35 of the Euratom Treaty, covering the period 1990‐1997, is being prepared. A recent deployment of new posts is a first step towards constituting a dedicated verification group that would be able to conduct more frequent verifications.

Question No 77 by James Fitzsimons (H−0821/98)

Subject: No confidence in UK nuclear industry

The UK's Health and Safety Executive stated on 1 September last that an audit of the Dounreay nuclear plant in northern Scotland has uncovered ‘many chronic safety problems'. It is no longer in a position to tackle its principal mission which is now to decommission the Dounreay site. There is also an overdependence on contractors for many functions. The UK Atomic Energy Authority has no comprehensive strategy for dealing with radioactive waste.

Taking into account the numerous accidents at the Sellafield complex and the failure to allay public disquiet about its safety and the ‘chronic safety problems' uncovered at the Dounreay plant in Scotland, will the Commission now make a statement on these matters?

Answer

The Commission has duly received a copy of the HSE report published on 1 September. It is an extensive document which will take some time to examine in detail before the Commission can decide whether any action would be appropriate. However, it can already be stated that the overall situation as revealed by the report is very disturbing.

Question No 78 by Christine Oddy (H−0760/98)

Subject: Cuba and the EU‐US Understanding on Cuba of 18 May 1998

Is the Commission aware that there is considerable concern over the recently agreed EU‐US Understanding over Cuba in that the Understanding applies only to the European Union and therefore by implication not to the rest of the world and that, in consequence, the sovereignty of Cuba is still being undermined by the Helms‐Burton legislation? What steps does the Commission intend to take to ensure that the UN decision for the immediate and unconditional lifting of the US blockade against Cuba is applied?

Answer

As indicated in replies to numerous written and oral questions in this issue, the EU/US Understanding with respect to disciplines for the strengthening of investment protection of 18 May 1998 goes beyond addressing the issue of possible illegal expropriations in Cuba and is not a multilateralisation of the Helms‐Burton Act. On the contrary, it is a valuable step forward in investment protection policy because it addresses the general issue of investment in illegal expropriations. This is very much in the Community's interest since Europe is the biggest outward investor world‐wide and by inhibiting and deterring investment in illegally expropriated property the protection of European investment world‐wide is enhanced.

The Understanding does not undermine the sovereignty of Cuba. What the Community has agreed to under the Understanding is a policy whereby Community investment in property, which the Commission is certain was illegally expropriated, is affected by the application of a set of agreed disciplines on the part of the Community and Member States. Thus, only future investment, by Community investors, in Cuba will be affected and only to the extent that it depends on official assistance from the Member States. The agreed disciplines will not amount to a ban of investment in Cuba and existing investment in Cuba, even if made in illegally expropriated property, is completely untouched by the Understanding.

With regard to the US blockade against Cuba, the Community continues to work towards a lifting thereof within the framework of the United Nations. As indicated in the Council Common Position on Cuba of December 1996, the Community is convinced that the best way to promote a smooth transition in Cuba is to maintain a constructive engagement with the authorities and to try to influence the evolution by concrete action rather than by a policy of isolation.

Question No 79 by Mihail Papayannakis (H−0761/98)

Subject: Transparency of financial relations between Member States and public enterprises

Public enterprises play a significant role in the Member States' national economies and, because of developments in the conditions under which competition takes place in the common market, there is a greater need for transparency in financial relations between public authorities and public enterprises. Moreover, as the Commission has established, substantial amounts of government aid are paid out illicitly.

With these considerations in mind, will the Commission say whether Greece has adopted the necessary provisions to incorporate Directives 80/723/EEC(17) and 93/84/EEC(18) into national law and, if so, when? Has Greece notified the Commission of the measures it has taken to comply with those directives and is Greece implementing the directives correctly?

Answer

The Commission would draw the attention of the honourable Member to the fact that, on 2 July last, the Greek authorities informed the Commission of the measures taken to incorporate Directive 80/723/EEC, as amended by Directive 93/84/EEC, into Greek law. These measures are contained in Article 27 of Law 2579.

However, no agreement has yet been reached on the obligations resulting from the directive.

As a result, the Commission has decided to await the supplementary information requested from the Greek authorities on 27 July last, before ruling on the possible closure of the infringement procedure (A/96/2253) opened in 1996 following noncompliance of information supplied with the requirements of the directive.

Question No 80 by Laura González Álvarez (H−0762/98)

Subject: Inappropriate use of EU funds in connection with the Parque Lineal del Manzanares project in Madrid

Question No 81 by Paul Lannoye (H−0766/98)

Subject: Doñana

Can the Commission indicate whether additional Structural Funds are being allocated to the Spanish authorities with regard to restoration plans for the Doñana ecosystems (e.g. Project 2005)?

What steps are being taken to ensure that these plans are implemented only after a sufficient clean‐up of the toxic sludge and water has been achieved and that the plans are consistent with the environmental objectives of the Sustainable Development Plan for the whole Doñana area, i.e. including the whole Natura 2000 site and not just the National Park?
