Question No 82 by Birgitta Ahlqvist (H−0768/98)

Subject: Cultural diversity in the Green Book on convergence

According to Article 128 of the Maastricht Treaty, the EU has the obligation to ‘contribute to the flowering of the cultures of the Member States, while respecting their national and regional diversity'. Furthermore, the EU shall, if necessary, support and supplement their action for instance where conservation and safeguarding of cultural heritage of European significance is concerned.

Diversity is an important part of the European cultural heritage. Can the Commission say which measures have been provided for in the Green Book on convergence to preserve cultural diversity?

Answer

The honourable Member has put the question of which measures have been foreseen in the Green Paper on Convergence to preserve cultural diversity.

It should be pointed out that no specific measures, on any aspect of convergence, were foreseen in the Green Paper. The purpose of the Green Paper was not to launch legislative or policy proposals, but to initiate as wide‐ranging a debate as possible on a new and complex phenomenon. It certainly succeeded in this objective. The Commission has summarised the results of the consultation process launched by the Green Paper in a Commission working document adopted on 29 July 1998.

The honourable Member will recall that the Green Paper contains a section specifically devoted to the question of meeting public interest objectives in the future. In this section, the promotion of cultural diversity is identified as one of these objectives. A great many of the responses to the Green Paper reaffirmed this position. However, the question raised by the Green Paper is how public interest objectives might be met in an environment where certain aspects of the telecoms, broadcasting and IT sectors have converged. The debate on this point is ongoing.

The Commission working document just mentioned also contains three further questions intended to deepen the convergence debate on certain key issues, namely: access to networks and digital gateways in a converging environment; creating the framework for investment, innovation, and encouraging European content production, distribution and availability; ensuring a balanced approach to regulation.

All these issues have implications for cultural diversity and the Commission will take these fully into account when it produces a communication on convergence towards the end of this year. This will contain further analysis as well as any policy proposals considered necessary.

Finally, the Commission can inform the honourable Member that it intends to carry out a specific action with regard to this question. It will shortly address a questionnaire to Member States, with a view to drawing up a Green Paper specifically focusing on the definition of appropriate European policies with regard to the cultural aspects of new audiovisual and information services.

Question No 83 by Anne McIntosh (H−0769/98)

Subject: Common Agricultural Policy

Will the Commission extend the concession on IACS registered land which was given to raspberry and blackcurrant growers to any other fruit growers, in particular to apple producers?

Answer

The compensatory payments granted by the Community to producers of certain arable crops are governed by Council Regulation (EEC) No 1765/92. Article 9 of this regulation defines the crops that cannot benefit from compensatory aid:

’Applications for the compensatory payment and set‐aside declarations may not be made in respect of land that on 31 December 1991 was under permanent pasture, permanent crops or trees or was used for non‐agricultural purposes."

This means that land devoted to permanent crops or permanent grass, which is the case for arboriculture and for dairy sector, at the 31 December 1991 is not eligible to compensatory aid.

In Annex I of Commission Regulation (CE) No 658/96, which establishes the implementing rules for granting compensatory payments, the following definition of permanent crops is given: ‘crops that occupy the ground for five years or longer and yield repeated harvests, with the exception of multi‐annual crops.

Among multi‐annual crops are included raspberries and blackberries to which the honourable Member referred.

This exception is clearly limited to crops which are characterized by a maximum five years rotation period. The Commission has no intention to extend the eligibility to compensatory payments, which is in principle reserved to arable land, to land devoted to permanent crops. Apple trees whose production period extends over a much longer period, form part of this last category and cannot benefit from compensatory aid.

Question No 84 by Arthur Newens (H−0770/98)

Subject: Report from the European Commission on the results and achievements of Directive 92/42 on energy efficiency

Article 10 of Council Directive 92/42(20) committed the European Commission to submitting a report to the European Parliament within three years of the Directive's implementation on its results and achievements. Since the Directive came into force in January 1993, will the Commission give an assurance that the report will be forthcoming before January 1999 – by which time it will be three years late?

Answer

Article 10 of Directive 92/42/EEC does indeed lay down that:

'Three years after the implementation of this Directive the Commission shall submit a report to the European Parliament and to the Council on the results achieved....'

However, Article 9(2) lays down that:

'Until 31 December 1997, Member States shall permit the placing on the market and putting into service of appliances complying with the national rules and schemes in force within their territories on the date of the adoption of this Directive.'

Most Member States have availed themselves of that leeway.

All the Member States have transposed the Community Directive into national law.
It is, however, too early to assess the results achieved.

Article 10 of the Directive goes on to lay down that:

'That report shall be accompanied by proposals for any changes to be made to this Directive in the light of those results and of advances in technology.'

Accordingly, the Commission does not envisage proposing any changes to the output requirements laid down in Article 5(1).

Question No 85 by Eva Kjer Hansen (H−0773/98)

Subject: Internal registration of documents at the Commission

The Court of Auditors special report on UCLAF reveals that no rules exist for the form and content of UCLAF's case files (3.13‐3.18) and cites a further example from the tourism sector where incriminating documents were systematically destroyed (2.33).

Can the Commission confirm that there is no set of rules applicable to the Commission's departments for the filing and registration of documents?

Will it introduce at an early date a set of rules for the filing and registration of documents so as to rule out the possibility of case files being arbitrarily destroyed in any of the Commission's departments?

Answer

The Commission can inform the honourable Member that rules on filing and storing documents at the Commission do exist. The main ones are as follows:

‐ the Commission Decision of 18 March 1986 on the handling of documents (SEC(86)388), which requires any document entering or leaving a Commission service to be identified (Article 3), recorded (Articles 5 and 7), filed (Article 6), and later, when it proves no longer necessary for the service to keep it in store, transferred to the historical archives (Article 10). This decision also requires each Director‐General or Head of Service to take the necessary measures to ensure its implementation (Article 13);

‐ the handbook on processing documents (April 1986), which lays down the procedures to be followed by the various services within the Commission to implement the above decision;

‐ Article 7 of Commission Decision 359/83/CECA and Council Regulation (EEC/EURATOM) No 354/83, which requires each institution to transfer all documents and other material from their current archives to their historical archives 15 years from the date of issue at the latest.

These rules apply to the Unit for the Coordination of Fraud Prevention. The Unit has central archives where a record of all documents entering or leaving the Unit is kept. A copy of each document is held in the archives in chronological order. The comments made by the Court of Auditors relate to the working files kept by investigators for their own purposes, and not to the general rules on filing documents.

Question No 86 by Alfred Lomas (H−0774/98)

Subject: No replies from the Commission to MEPs' letters

In view of the Commission's earlier promise to provide replies to MEPs within 3 weeks, can the Commission explain why no response or even acknowledgement has been made, despite reminders, to letters I wrote to the Secretary‐General on 4 June, with a request that it be forwarded to the appropriate Fisheries Commissioner, and on 6 May to Directorate‐General X?

Answer

While the Commission certainly aims to reply to MEPs' letters within three weeks and has in place a system which guarantees such reply in most cases, it obviously is unable to do so in all cases.

The honourable Member's letter of 4 June 1998 received a reply on 28 July 1998 that is before the honourable Member's question was tabled on 7 August 1998.

The Commission Secretary general's letter of 28 July 1998 already expressed regret at the delay in giving the reply due to the technically complicated nature of the dossier.

Unfortunately the Commission can find no trace of the honourable Member's letter of 6 May 1998 and he is invited to send that letter again.

The honourable Member is reminded that DG X runs a help desk for MEPs' inquiries.

Question No 87 by Felipe Camisón Asensio (H−0777/98)

Subject: Survey of international telephone charges

Why has the Commission launched a survey concerning the international telephone charges applied by certain operators in the European Union?

Answer

At present, prices for telephone calls across borders are generally much higher than prices for calls within a single Member State. Although liberalisation of the Community telecoms markets allows other systems to be used, an important proportion of cross‐border calls within the Community is still forwarded through the traditional accounting rates system – which amount to transfer prices between operators – based on bilateral agreements between operators.

Originally, these accounting rates were set at a level intended to cover the total cost of transporting the telephone call. Nowadays, as a result of technological changes and consequent reductions in costs, it is widely believed that these charges no longer reflect the true cost of calls. As these accounting rates have an important influence on the prices users pay for international phone calls, reductions in accounting rates should lead to substantial price reductions for consumers in the future. Though the investigation does not focus on the end‐user international prices, it is indirectly relevant to these prices. The interest of users in the Community – both consumers and business – demands that the price of international telephone calls be brought down to a fair and adequate level.

At present, two set of policies require that accounting rates (or their future substitute) charged by major European operators be cost‐orientated: under competition rules, the prohibition of abuses of dominant positions(21) , and under harmonisation rules, the ‘Interconnection directive"(22) . The Commission is therefore scrutinising the current accounting rates arrangements very carefully with a view to furthering this goal of cost‐orientation.

Question No 88 by Manuel Medina Ortega (H−0779/98)

Subject: Seizure of a Community vessel in Gambia

What measures will the Commission take to secure the release of the Community fishing vessel BRIZ III, which together with its crew has been illegally seized and detained by Gambia for more than two months, and to bring to account the Government of Gambia for the ill‐treatment of the crew, illegal seizure of the vessel and other breaches of international law, in order to ensure that incidents of this kind do not recur in future?

Answer

The Commission would draw the honourable Member's attention to the fact that, at the time it was boarded, the Briz III was fishing in Gambian waters under the Senegalese flag. From a legal point of view, it was not therefore part of the Community's fleet when the incident took place.

Moreover, since the fishing agreement between the Community and Gambia is suspended at present, the European Commission is not in a position to intervene with the Gambian authorities.

Nevertheless, the Commission immediately relayed the Spanish authorities" concerns about the conditions under which the crew of the vessel were being detained to the Gambian ambassador in Brussels. The members of the crew held on the vessel were later released and have since returned to Spain.

Question No 89 by Ioannis Theonas (H−0782/98)

Subject: Pressure for the immediate ending of the OTE telecommunications monopoly

In a recent letter, the Commission stated that it would suspend the payment of Community funds of 70 billion drachmas intended for the OTE under an operational programme for telecommunications unless the Greek Government made rapid progress with the legislative and other measures needed to end the OTE monopoly in accordance with Community legislation on telecommunications.

Bearing in mind that the transitional period fixed for the full liberalization of telecommunications in Greece runs until 1 January 2001, does the Commission not consider that actions of this kind run counter to the spirit of the relevant legislative provisions, while assisting efforts to liberalize the market immediately and allow access to new service providers before the end of the transitional period, thus damaging the position of the OTE to the benefit of specific private interests?

Answer

Article 4 of the Commission Decision of 18 December 1995 on the granting of financial aid from the Structural Funds to the ‘Telecommunications" operational programme (OP) requires the Greek authorities to comply with the timetable they themselves had put forward for the harmonisation of Greek rules in this area. Failure to do so would result in the payment of Community appropriations being suspended.

Having granted several extensions totalling more than two years beyond the period allowed in the timetable, the Commission found that the Community directives on telecommunications had not been incorporated correctly into Greek law. In its letter of 12 August 1998, the Commission informed the Greek authorities of its decision to suspend the payments under this programme. In fact, eleven cases of infringement had been recorded, resulting in seven letters of formal complaint, one reasoned Opinion and one sentence handed down by the Court of Justice.

The derogation concerning the OTE (Greek telecommunications service) monopoly of the public telephone system is not being called into question. However, under Article 155 of the Treaty, the Commission must ensure that Community directives in this sector are applied. This is not the case in Greece at present, as a large number of infringements in this area have been recorded by the Commission.

The Commission can state that, since it was adopted, this OP has absorbed ECU 141 million, or 70 % of the funds allocated. The suspension of payments only relates to unutilised appropriations, totalling approximately ECU 63 million.

Question No 90 by Ulla Sandbæk (H−0787/98)

Subject: EU subsidised milk scheme

Efforts are made throughout the EU to ensure a good overall level of public health. But does the Commission realise that one of the factors hampering those efforts seems to be the current scheme for subsidising school milk? At present, the subsidy is available only for milk in which the percentage of energy derived from fat exceeds the recommended maximum of 30 %, and hence not for nutritionally sound products such as skimmed milk and buttermilk. This obviously poses a problem as regards health. In Denmark, however, a great deal is being done to ensure that school‐age children acquire healthy eating habits. For instance, criteria have been introduced in a good many school canteens whereby the total intake of fat must not exceed the recommended 30 %. It is therefore very regrettable indeed that the EU subsidised school milk scheme actually counteracts the efforts that are being made to ensure that schoolchildren have healthy, low‐fat diets.

Will the Commission state why the subsidy is available only for high‐fat products, and does it plan to amend this nutritionally unsound scheme?

Answer

Whilst it is accepted that a general reduction in fat consumption would contribute to public health, this does not require milk fat to be entirely absent from the diet of school children. Against this background, and given that the school milk programme is part of the common market regime for milk, which supports both the fat and the protein content of milk, it does not seem appropriate to use this programme as a means of encouraging the consumption of fat free milk by children.

Moreover, since the objective of the programme is to promote the consumption of milk, it is important to pay attention to the taste of dairy products offered. Milk fat is an essential element in this respect. Regarding semi‐skimmed milk, in particular, it is difficult to accept that this product should be qualified as rich in fat.

The Commission considers that the present range of eligible dairy products is suitable for achieving a varied and balanced diet for school aged children and is, therefore, not envisaging amending it. Finally, as regards the 30 % rule mentioned by the honourable member, it would seem that this rule should be applied to the overall food intake rather than to individual products.

Question No 92 by Antonios Trakatellis (H−0794/98)

Subject: Failure by the Commission to take part in the International Exhibition in Thessaloniki

Why has the Commission decided not to have a stand at this year's International Exhibition in Thessaloniki after doing so continuously for the past 15 years (1983‐1997)?

What are the reasons behind this decision, particularly in the light of Thessaloniki's increasingly prominent role in the Balkan region (it was European City of Culture for 1997) and the fact that during this year's exhibition the Greek Minister for Public Works, Regional Planning and the Environment is giving details of the major projects being implemented or planned in Greece, particularly northern Greece, to be financed with Community resources?

Question No 93 by Georgios Anastassopoulos (H−0795/98)

Subject: Failure by the Commission to participate in the International Exhibition in Thessaloniki

Why for the first time in 15 years was there no stand representing the Commission at this year's International Exhibition in Thessaloniki?

How can this decision be squared with the growing importance of this exhibition in the Balkans region, an area which has constantly been of major concern to the European Union in recent years?

Joint answer

The Commission has indeed participated over the past 14 years in the International Fair of Thessaloniki (IFT), which has a growing importance in the region. In 1997, the Commission and the European Parliament enjoyed the use of 2 separate but adjoining stands with a common entrance, representing the European Union as a whole.

These stands were generally considered to be prime locations at the Fair, thus reflecting the importance the Commission attached to its presence at IFT. It had been the Commission's intention to participate on the same basis at the 63rd IFT.

On 2 February of this year, IFT's organisers, HELEXPO, wrote to the Commission seeking to know its intentions to participate. On 25 February, the Commission replied that it wished to participate on the same basis as in 1977, using the same exhibition space and in the same location.

However, on 5 May, IFT's organisers informed the Commission to the effect that the usual prime location was not available for it and that a different location was being offered. This new location was considered by the Commission to offer less visibility, and a reply in this respect was sent on 6 May, indicating that, if the prime location and its space which had been traditionally allocated over the past 14 years were not made available to it then the Commission's participation at the 63rd IFT would have to be reconsidered.

It is understood that a similar letter was sent to IFT's organisers by the European Parliament.

However, no written rely to this letter of 6 May was received by the Commission. After various attempts to contact IFT's organisers, the President of HELEXPO indicated orally on 22 May that he was unable to change the decision on the new location.

In the light of this, the Commission wrote on 25 May to IFT's organisers indicating that it would not be able to participate in the 63rd IFT. No reply was received from IFT's organisers to this letter, although the President of HELEXPO did telephone the Commission at the beginning of July to see if it maintained its decision.

It is noted that on 15 June the European Parliament wrote again to IFT's organisers agreeing to accept the less visible location and therefore to participate in the 63rd IFT.

It is to be hoped that the Commission will be able to participate in next year's IFT on the basis of its previous participations.

Question No 94 by Jean‐Antoine Giansily (H−0801/98)

Subject: Implementation of the 1998 budget

Within the budget for the financial year 1998, the Commission has hitherto not implemented Amendment 1 to the institutions' administrative expenditure, Chapter A ‘Staff in active employment', concerning ‘permanent posts in the Supply Agency' in connection with ‘the duties of Deputy Director‐General of the Supply Agency'.

Can the Commission, whose task is to implement the European Union's budget and not to reform it, say precisely why it is refusing to implement a sovereign decision of one arm of the budgetary authority which simply applies Article 53 of the Treaty establishing the European Atomic Energy Community to the letter and has no financial implications?

Does the Commission think that it apposite to cite reasons of internal administrative convenience to justify its failure to act?

Answer

The Commission would like to draw the attention of the honourable Member of Parliament to the Commission's rely given on 8 July 1998 to the Priority Written Question (P−1915/98 Fr.) tabled by the honourable Member on the same subject matter and to confirm that, in the meantime, no developments have occurred.

Question No 95 by Alexandros Alavanos (H−0805/98)

Subject: Programme to prevent forest fires in Greece

The recent forest fires in Greece have caused the wholesale destruction of woodland and farming areas, as well as substantial damage to housing and infrastructure. What measures are provided for in the projects submitted by Greece under Regulation 308/97(23) on protection of the Community's forests against fire and how effective are they, given that in the current year, even more so than in the past, these measures have proved totally inadequate in preventing and containing fires? Do these projects provide for participation by the rural population in drawing up regional fire prevention action plans, in practising preventive silviculture and in primary intervention methods, as apparently required by Regulation 308/97? To what extent is the Commission prepared to contribute to financing reafforestation measures and programmes for fire damaged areas from the Cohesion Fund, as was done in Spain, where in 1995 alone 12 % of the appropriations allocated for environmental projects involved measures to regenerate vegetation in ecosystems damaged by fires?

Answer

The global plan for the protection of forests drawn up by Greece as part of the Community action to prevent forest fires includes a range of measures to raise public awareness, set up preventive equipment, introduce preventive practices into forestry and monitor forests, in cooperation with the various partners involved, including the rural population. This plan brings together all the efforts of the Greek Government to provide protection against forest fires. The Commission has granted aid of ECU 18 million to 79 Greek projects for the period 1992‐1998. These are contributing to the implementation of the global plan and relate to the measures outlined above. A general appraisal of the effectiveness of these measures will be made available in the near future, thanks to the results of the Community information system on forest fires set up under this Community action.

The EAGGF also supports forestry measures, with a total contribution by the Community under the regional operational programmes for the period 1994‐1999 of ECU 176.4 million. These measures may include replacing damaged forests and protecting the ground against erosion. If the Greek authorities wish to reafforest some of the areas affected by the recent fires, they could therefore do so under these measures by redeploying funds, so long as sufficient resources are available. As regards the Cohesion Fund, in 1994 the Commission has granted aid of ECU 12 million for measures to protect forests and the land, and for reafforestation. In addition, the Commission is currently considering a request for ECU 31.5 million from the Cohesion Fund for fire‐fighting and monitoring equipment in Greece.

Question No 96 by Tony Cunningham (H−0806/98)

Subject: European Union steel industry

As the ripples from the Asian crisis spread ever wider, with the latest victims being Russia and Latin America, and with threats of economic instability reaching also to China, how does the Commission react to the voices in the USA calling for increased trade protection from imports of steel products? Does the Commission not agree that closing off the USA market to steel imports will only add to the problem by exacerbating over‐supply problems in the rest of the world, attracting additional imports to the EU and pushing world steel prices still further downwards?

Answer

The Commission is aware that the US steel industry, like the Community steel industry, has expressed concern to its authorities about recent import trends. The Commission is not aware that the US Administration has so far received any specific requests for protective action.

No doubt the US Administration, like the Commission, is currently monitoring the consequences of the Asian crisis, including the implementation by countries directly affected by the financial crisis of the programmes of reform agreed with the IMF, the World Bank and the Asian Development Bank.

Question No 97 by Roger Barton (H−0807/98)

Subject: European Union steel industry

In the context of enlargement, can the Commission outline the path it intends to follow to ensure that candidate countries will fully respect the requirement to restructure their steel industries prior to accession? What actions will the Commission take, and when, if it appears that they will not meet the timetable for restructuring?

Answer

The Commission policy on the steel sector in the candidate countries has been developed in its communication of 7 April 1998 (COM(1998)220 final). In this Communication, the Commission has argued for:

‐ the speeding up of the restructuring and privatisation process of the steel sector in the applicant countries;

‐ a concerted and integrated approach both at regional and transnational levels.

The Commission is ready to support and facilitate this process through the co‐financing of socio‐regional measures. It will also help mobilising resources from the International Financial Institutions.

The candidate countries should adopt comprehensive restructuring plans and respect the Europe Agreements, in particular as regards state aids conditions. The Commission will monitor closely the timely implementation of the restructuring plans.

Question No 98 by David Bowe (H−0808/98)

Subject: European steel industry

Due to the downturn in Asian markets there has been an explosion of imports of steel products on to European Union markets. For example, in the first three to four months of this year, imports of ECSC steel products have increased by 65 %. These imports have exceeded by far the development of consumption and far outstrip the possibilities of absorption by the market. Does the Commission agree that this could have serious implications for the EU steel industry and for the European Union's industrial policy? What measures does the Commission envisage to provide relief to the industry?

Answer

The Commission is monitoring developments and is aware of the concerns of the Community steel industry, which is understood to be considering anti‐dumping or anti‐subsidy complaints. If the industry presents complaints, these will be examined on their merits in accordance with the relevant Community legislation.

Question No 99 by Michael Tappin (H−0809/98)

Subject: European Union steel industry

For the first time ever, the EU has a negative trade balance in steel. This is due to a combination of increased imports from third countries for which their domestic and traditional export markets have reduced demand, and reduced EU exports. Does the Commission consider that this is a satisfactory situation for long term stability of employment and prosperity in the EU steel industry?

Answer

The Commission is monitoring developments and is aware of the concerns of the Community steel industry with respect to the possible consequences for the long‐term stability of employment and prosperity in the Community steel industry. At this stage, however, it is impossible to assess how the steel trade trends during the first three months of 1998 will affect the Community's trade balance for 1998 as a whole or whether there will be any serious damage to employment and profitability of the Community steel industry.

Question No 100 by Linda McAvan (H−0810/98)

Subject: European Union steel industry

Is the Commission aware that in response to the economic crisis in Asia, the EU has in recent months seen large quantities of imported steel arriving from source countries that have hitherto not been traditional suppliers? In many cases, these new imports have arrived in large quantities into a single region within the EU, threatening the stability of the market for steel in that area, which in turn threatens the stability of the steel market throughout the EU. In view of the possibility that the EU economy may itself begin to slow down as the consequence of the Asian problems, what action does the Commission propose to protect the EU steel industry and its employees?

Answer

The Commission is aware that imports of steel into the Community have been increasing in recent months in response to the financial crisis in S.E. Asia. Certain countries in that region have begun exporting large quantities of steel to the Community for the first time and countries that are no longer able to export their steel products to Asia have been increasing their exports to the Community.

The Commission is monitoring the consequences of the Asian crisis, including the implementation by countries directly affected by the financial crisis of the programmes of reform agreed with the IMF, the World Bank and the Asian Development Bank. ASEM leaders undertook at their meeting in April 1998 not to take any restrictive measures in the legitimate exercise of their WTO rights that would go beyond that which is necessary to remedy specific situations, as provided for in WTO rules.

If the Community steel industry presents anti‐dumping or anti‐subsidy complaints, these will be examined on their merits in accordance with the relevant Community legislation.

Question No 101 by Vassilis Ephremidis (H−0812/98)

Subject: Adverse effects on agriculture in the Member States of the projected EU agreement with Mercosur

The projected agreement on the establishment of a free trade area with the countries of Mercosur has provoked serious concerns and sharp reactions in many Community countries, along with fears that the agreement may generate serious problems for European agriculture, leading to a situation where Community farm products are supplanted by products from the countries of South America.

Given the massive problems faced by farmers in the EU Member States, particularly in the south and in the Mediterranean products sector, does the Commission intend to go ahead with negotiations which will further exacerbate the position of European farmers at a time when a fresh round of WTO negotiations is due to begin and further reforms to the CAP are planned with a view to enlargement?

Answer

As indicated in the Council"s recommendation for a decision authorising the Commission to negotiate an inter‐regional agreement between the European Community and Mercosur for the agriculture and fisheries sector, the Commission will take into account the threat which this presents for certain products. During the negotiations, it will ensure that setting up a free trade area does not have an adverse effect on existing policies.

The impact assessment study carried out by the Commission identified as problem areas those imports from Mercosur which could affect the common agricultural policy (some of these products being covered by common organisations of the market). The beef sector, certain cereals and sugar are good examples.

The impact assessment study drew up a list of threatened products which included a number of fresh fruits. However, the difference in the seasons should reduce any negative impact.

During the negotiations, the Commission will work to ensure that any undertakings it gives are compatible with CAP mechanisms in their present form, or as they will stand following any changes made before the end of the negotiations.

Question No 103 by Brian Crowley (H−0822/98)

Subject: Lessons of the 1998 football World Cup

Now that the football World Cup is over, what lessons does the Commission believe have been learned in terms of dealing with football hooliganism and in countering the sale of counterfeit football sports wear and other goods?

Answer

Within the framework of the Council of Justice and Home Affairs, the Commission is supporting actions being undertaken by police forces to examine experience collated during the 1998 World Cup for the purpose of preventing hooliganism, and, generally speaking, the issue of violence occurring at sporting events.

The Commission is of course in favour of the promotion and the implementation of a closer police cooperation at a European level and it has been supporting, in the context of the OISIN programme, a series of experts' seminars on policing sporting events.

Insofar as counterfeit football sportswear and other goods are imported from third countries, a Community regulation enables the customs authorities to intervene. Internal enforcement is a matter for national law.

As regards counterfeit of goods in general the Commission is preparing a Green Paper seeking to address this issue, as well as the matter of piracy, in a global way within the internal market.

This Green Paper could lead to legislative initiatives but also to actions in order, for instance, to improve cooperation between enterprises and public authorities.

Question No 104 by Gerard Collins (H−0823/98)

Subject: Removing EU import duty on aluminium

According to statistics produced by the European Aluminium Association, production of aluminium in Western Europe could not keep pace with soaring demand in 1997.

Does the Commission now consider that the European Union's import duty on aluminium is still justified in view of the demand for and growth in the use of aluminium in a range of sectors including transport, building, construction industries, packaging cars, fast ferries and aircraft?

Answer

The current Community import duty on primary aluminium was established in the context of previous GATT tariff negotiations. It is not ruled out that this duty could be revised in the context of the next round of WTO multilateral trade negotiations.

Regarding the growing reliance of Community manufacturers on imported aluminium highlighted by the honourable MEP, it must be underlined that only a part of Community imports of primary aluminium is subject to the import duty.

As a result of several preferential trade agreements concluded by the EC with third countries, a large number of aluminium producers can export aluminium to the EC free of duty. These countries include very important aluminium producing countries such as Norway, Central and Eastern European countries, ACP countries, which account for more than 50 % of total Community imports of primary aluminium.

Moreover, imports for inward processing into the EC are also exempt from import duties.

In summary, in 1997, only 29 % of primary aluminium imports into the EC paid customs duties.

Question No 105 by Shaun Spiers (H−0824/98)

Subject: Measles, Mumps and Rubella Vaccine

There is concern in the UK about the possible link between the Measles, Mumps and Rubella vaccine (MMR) and Crohn's Disease or Autism.

What research into the MMR vaccine has the Commission considered?

What is the situation regarding the MMR vaccine in other Member States?

Answer

Concern about the potential risks associated with children's vaccinations, especially Crohn's disease and autism as alleged possible side‐effects of MMR vaccines, has recently been raised by a paper in the scientific journal The Lancet and was discussed on the occasion of Oral Question No H−507/98 asked by the honourable Member Per Gahrton.

Vaccine is the most powerful preventive measure against infectious diseases. The prevailing scientific opinion is that there is no evidence which warrants the abandonment of the combined vaccine against measles, mumps and rubella, given its benefits.

The Commission is not aware of any changes in the vaccination policies of Member States as a result of recent discussions in scientific circles.

Under the terms of Chapter Va of Council Directive 75/319/EEC on the monitoring of proprietary medicinal products, Member States which have authorised the placing on the market of vaccines, including those for use on children, are responsible for implementing vaccine monitoring measures.

No project on this specific issue is currently included under Community‐financed research programmes.

Nevertheless, research into infectious diseases and vaccines will be included in the key action 2 ‘Control of Infectious Diseases" within the specific programme ‘Quality of Life and Management of Living Resources" of the 5th Framework Programme for Research and Technological Development.(24)

Question No 106 by Inger Schörling (H−0829/98)

Subject: Mobile slaughterhouses

There are no facilities in northern Sweden for slaughtering laying hens. This is because Sweden's most northerly poultry slaughterhouse is in Skara, and it is not considered economical to transport laying hens to it from further north than Gästrikland. This means that laying hens from more northerly areas cannot be slaughtered and instead have to be destroyed using anti‐simex, at a cost of SEK 2 per hen. Plans have therefore been put forward for a mobile slaughterhouse which could travel between egg producers in northern Sweden and slaughter laying hens which were not longer suitable for egg production purposes. This would be justified on animal welfare grounds, as the hens would not have to be transported at all, and also on economic grounds, as the meat could then be used. However, Sweden's National Food Administration has been informed by an official at the Commission that mobile slaughterhouses are not permitted. Is it true that mobile slaughterhouses can never be permitted? If so, how does the Commission consider that the above problem might be resolved? Should the rules not take account of the circumstances that prevail in remote areas? What are the Commission's views as regards the animal welfare and economic impact of the arrangements described above?

Answer

In principle economic grounds do not govern the application of public health regulations in the frame of preventive actions for the protection of consumers.

Directive 71/118/EEC (’Directive for fresh poultrymeat") lays down the health requirements for the slaughter of poultry in the Community. The directive (Articles 3 and 7) already contains provisions to take account of the problems of small production systems.

In this context certain derogations are already possible under this Directive from certain structural and infrastructure requirements. Farmers with an annual production of under 10 000 birds may be derogated by the Member States from general requirements as well as poultry slaughterhouses handling under 150 000 birds per year.

The extent of the derogations and the criteria for their application are laid down in the Directive and the competent national authority should determine on spot whether the mobile units can fulfil the requirements. This will always depend on the individual case (throughput per time unit, supply of water, refrigeration equipments etc.) and must be judged on a case by case basis.

The Commission is certainly open for new developments in technology. The request should be brought to the attention of the Commission as provided by Community legislation so that it can be considered the conformity of these mobile slaughter units with the rules of Directive 71/118/EEC.

Question No 107 by James Nicholson (H−0831/98)

Subject: European pig industry

Considering the present difficulties being experienced by pig farmers throughout the European Union, will the Commission give consideration to the proposal to introduce aid for private storage, or export restitution refunds and are any other proposals being considered to assist the European pig industry?

Answer

After two years of extraordinary profitable conditions for pigmeat producers, 1996 and 1997, it is not surprising if European pig farmers have increased their herds. It is estimated that production in 1998 will increase by around 5 % compared to 1997, which represents an extra quantity of 800 000 tonnes.

Due to this substantial increase, pig prices in the European Community decreased over the last six months, showing clearly the cyclical nature of pig production. Every pig producer should be aware of this and therefore be able to take it into account as a risk factor.

The common organisation of the market in pigmeat gives the Commission only limited possibilities to support the pigmeat market. Export refunds and aid for private storage are the only two instruments. By introducing export refunds for pig carcasses and bone‐in cuts in May 1998, the Commission has taken action to support the market. On 3 August 1998 those export refunds have been increased by 50 % and new products became eligible for refunds.

The introduction of aid for private storage is at present under consideration by the Commission. The timing of such a measure is very important in order to avoid that pigmeat comes back on the market before the market is in a better shape.

No other proposals are being considered at present because of the lack of a legal basis.
